# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

## Chapter 9: Lower Most Mantle and D:




### Section: 9.3 Phase Transitions and Heterogeneity in the Lowermost Mantle:

The lowermost mantle, also known as the D'' layer, is a region of the Earth's mantle that extends from a depth of approximately 2900 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2890 km. This layer is characterized by extreme pressure and temperature conditions, making it a challenging region to study. However, recent advancements in mineral physics and geophysics have provided valuable insights into the structure and composition of the lowermost mantle.

#### 9.3a Phase Transitions in the Lowermost Mantle

One of the most significant features of the lowermost mantle is the post-perovskite phase transition. This phase transition occurs at a depth of approximately 660 km and is characterized by a change in the crystal structure of the mantle material from a cubic to an orthorhombic lattice. The post-perovskite phase is stable at higher pressures and temperatures than perovskite, making it the dominant phase in the lowermost mantle.

The post-perovskite phase transition is of particular importance as it is believed to be responsible for the seismic discontinuities observed in the D'' layer. These discontinuities, known as the D'''' layer, are characterized by a sharp increase in seismic velocity and density. The post-perovskite phase transition is thought to be the cause of these discontinuities, as the change in crystal structure leads to a change in the physical properties of the mantle material.

The post-perovskite phase transition is also of interest due to its potential role in mantle convection. The positive Clapeyron slope of the post-perovskite phase transition means that as the temperature increases, the pressure at which the phase transition occurs also increases. This could have significant implications for the dynamics of mantle convection, as the post-perovskite phase transition could act as a barrier to the movement of mantle material.

#### 9.3b Heterogeneity in the Lowermost Mantle

In addition to the post-perovskite phase transition, the lowermost mantle is also characterized by significant heterogeneity in its composition. This heterogeneity is believed to be a result of the complex processes that occur in the lowermost mantle, including chemical reactions and melting.

One of the most significant sources of heterogeneity in the lowermost mantle is the presence of ultra-low velocity zones (ULVZs). These regions, characterized by a decrease in seismic velocity, are believed to be regions of partial melt or highly viscous material. The presence of ULVZs in the lowermost mantle has been linked to the formation of hotspots and plumes, as well as the movement of mantle material.

Another source of heterogeneity in the lowermost mantle is the presence of iron. Recent studies have shown that iron may make up as much as 15% of the lowermost mantle, with concentrations varying significantly throughout the layer. The presence of iron is believed to be a result of the iron-rich composition of the core and the mixing of core and mantle material at the core-mantle boundary.

#### 9.3c Implications of Phase Transitions and Heterogeneity

The phase transitions and heterogeneity in the lowermost mantle have significant implications for the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. The post-perovskite phase transition, for example, could act as a barrier to mantle convection, leading to changes in the dynamics of the Earth's interior. The presence of ULVZs and iron in the lowermost mantle also suggests complex processes occurring in this region, which could have implications for the formation of hotspots and plumes.

Furthermore, the study of phase transitions and heterogeneity in the lowermost mantle could provide insights into the composition and evolution of the Earth's core. The presence of iron in the lowermost mantle, for example, could be a result of mixing between the core and mantle, providing insights into the processes occurring at the core-mantle boundary.

In conclusion, the lowermost mantle is a complex and dynamic region of the Earth's interior. The study of phase transitions and heterogeneity in this region is crucial for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Further research in this area will continue to provide valuable insights into the processes occurring in the lowermost mantle and their implications for the Earth as a whole.
